RATING: 9
This game proves why Werewolf and its kin are bad games.  This game uses very similar mechanics.  It is a bluffing and negotiation game without any real way to actually deduce who is telling the truth and who isn't.  Much like Werewolf unless you are really good at interrogating people or an absolutely horrible liar a lot of the game boils down to blind guessing on who is telling the truth or not.

So, in more detail, the game has each player playing a merchant trying to get goods into Nottingham from Sherwood Forest.  They do this by secretly putting a certain number of cards in a bag and declaring what is in there.  Each player is only "allowed" to bring in one type of legal good each round.  There are 4 legal goods and a bunch of illegal contraband.  You probably won't win playing the whole game honestly bringing in exactly what you say.  The illegal contraband is worth many more points and there won't be many turns where you have a decent number of just one of the 4 legal goods to bring in.

So that's where the bluffing and negotiation come in.  After you declare what you have to the Sheriff he can interrogate each player and decide which bags to open or let pass.  If a player was lying about anything they lose those goods and pay a penalty (getting to keep what they were telling the truth about) but if they were telling the truth and are inspected the Sheriff pays that player the penalty.

Players can also offer the Sheriff bribes to let them pass.  This is where this becomes such a better game than Werewolf despite boiling down to the same blind guessing as in that game.  There are so many more decision trees involved.  Do you stay confident that you have legal goods hoping the Sheriff lets you pass?  Do you entice him to inspect your bag by offering paltry sums so he pays you a penalty?  Do you outright find a mutually beneficial agreement?  Do you lie about what you have in your bag to give him to get by for free?  Do you negotiate to attack a different player or for future un-binding favors?

And even past the current round you build up an identity and other decisions trees.  That player has been honest for several rounds and any Sheriff that investigated him have paid a price...but is he telling the truth this round?  That player has sneaked by so much contraband, is he continuing his criminal ways or is the heat too hot now and he's laying low?

On top of all that this is a very accessible game and plays reasonably quickly, even with the max player count of 5.  I do recommend playing simultaneously when possible and instituting the time limit for the Sheriff equal to one minute times the number of players.  We only ever hit the time limit once or twice during the whole game but even knowing there is one has a hurrying effect on the Sheriff.

The only problems I can see with this game result in who you play with.  If you have players that just won't "play the game" or get into it then it can be kind of a drag.  For example, we had one player in our first game that outright refused to pay for passage.  This made trying to negotiate with him a little dull and a bit more uninteresting.  We had a nice mix besides him so he didn't really bring down our game but having multiple players like that could definitely sour the experience I think.  Still otherwise a very good game.

RATING: 8.5
I love games that are fresh and new, those that don't reuse the same mechanics as a hundred other games while only changing the theme. I also love games that pit players against each other and bring in a heavy dose of psychological warfare. Sheriff of Nottingham is just that. It is a surprisingly simple yet incredibly fun game of power, maneuvering, deal-making, and strategy where a quiet introvert can beat a type-A aggressor at his own game! 

Play as merchants intent on bringing as many goods into the market as possible. Legal goods like apples, cheese, bread, and chickens bring a meager income while illegal contraband like pepper, mead, silk, and the coveted crossbow will rake in the coins...but at a substantial price if you get caught by the ever-present sheriff.  

Here's my thought on the strategy: on one end of the spectrum, you have players (usually those who are more conservative and honest at heart!) who stick to almost solely importing legal goods. They bring in a smaller yet consistent income. Beware of these kinds of players, however, because they will act the sweet, innocent merchant then turn around a 5-card sack of contraband! Can you tell I've been burned by them before? On the other side, you have the aggressive, get-rich-quick player who most often has at least a contraband or two in their sack but is willing to pay the Sheriff a hefty price to not take a peek. Each strategy works, but I've found that a good, unpredictable balance works best. 

The artwork is outright impressive. The colors are vibrant and fun which makes for a beautiful game with very high-quality components. For me, this adds immensely to the theme and, ultimately, to my liking or disliking of a game. 

The replay value is very high. The game itself changes moderately each time, but the most fun change is playing with different people. You'll find yourself playing an entirely different game with a completely different strategy depending on the type of people you're playing with. I've played games full of conservative people where bags are checked frequently and the winning score is relatively low. I've also played games with plenty of high-risk, high-return types who gamble for big gains (and big losses!). Usually, you'll have a good mix of personality types which makes for a very fun, rounded game. 

Overall, I give Sheriff of Nottingham a very high 8.5. This is an excellent game for all types of gamers, experienced or not. Those of us "game nerds" who love the intensely strategic games really enjoy it, but I also played with several very moderate gamers who absolutely loved it. Very well done!

RATING: 4
Players take turns playing the sheriff, who can be bluffed or bribed into letting each other player's goods pass uninspected, or can open up the bag and take a look -- if the hapless traveller is innocent, the sheriff pays a penalty, but a caught smuggler must pay to get out of trouble and loses their contraband to boot.

The only "social deduction" games I can stomach have more deduction and less social in them than this one; there is too little information for either the bluff or negotiation to really work. It's particularly annoying that the original theme of modern smuggling -- with neat little suitcases in the Hart an der Grenze version! -- was changed to this nonsense.

The length is a little much, too, if you get into the negotiation. And if you don't, why play? Far better off playing something like Coup or In a Grove where the player dynamics comes to the fore.

RATING: 8
Social deduction game of the Sheriff - players have to decide as the sheriff who is lying and who is telling the truth sheriff can take bribes to not inspect a bribe. If the person is completely honest than the sheriff has to pay a penalty but if not then the player getting inspected has to. Players can keep all the declared legal items in the bag. At the end scoring is done based on legal items as well as number of contraband. Players have to decide how much a bribe would be worth to not get discovered or they can bribe to screw over other players as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
How fun is it to be greedy, bribe the Sheriff, smuggle illegal items through the gates of town, and get crowned the Chicken King?  Pretty dang fun. Over before you want it to be, tense, tricky, and doublecrossing, this is probably the best social deduction game you can buy.  

Everyone takes turns playing the Sheriff, which means you are responsible for managing all the goods going to market and checking for certain things King John thinks shouldn't be in the hands of the commoners (fine linens, exotic spices, CROSSBOWS, golden apples, etc).  The other players take their goods to market in their merchant bags and have to pass an inspection made by the Sheriff...by any means necessary. Bribing and lying to the Sheriff to get him to allow your goods through WITHOUT an inspection is very common, and definitely makes the game what it is.  The only people that I've seen not enjoy this game are those that just can't handle the mental trickery and bribing.  I highly recommend this one.

RATING: 3.7
I can see why people like it, but good lord am I terrible at bluffing. Probably a 7-8 for someone who likes the game type. I'd personally avoid it just because my play is probably worse than random.

Still, I enjoy it more than other games of its genre, because the bluffing is done primarily 1-on-1, and because the setting and mechanics allow for entertaining nonsense if you're not really in contention for a win. (That is, there's no social obligation to take things too seriously, unlike other bluffing games where you're probably letting two or three other players down with your own weakness. Because each direct negotiation happens exactly 4 times, everyone has an equal chance to exploit how bad you are.)

RATING: 9.8
This game should be considered a classic, due it's simplicity, accessibility, uniqueness and brilliance of play. I often wonder why more games haven't tried to follow in the footsteps of Sheriff. The timed negotiation invokes tense discussion, the bluffing and deal making is well contained yet also open to good possibilities, and the app is a hilarious addition to the game. It has all the hallmarks of a great bluffing game. It's just shy of perfection due to the balancing issue, but even that can be thwarted with meta gaming, and may also be heavily improved upon with the expansion (which could change my rating).

RATING: 4
Not bad, but it seems like a good strategy is to just be honest and bring in sets of legal goods every turn. I've seen it happen at least once, and I've read online that it happens quite a bit. The game is also very mathy and it's possible to give somebody the win if players aren't paying attention and are just making offers without thinking it through. If you offer the sheriff a lot of money to open someone's bag, and the sheriff then finds contraband, they have usually made a ton of money with pretty much no risk.
My biggest issue is that there just isn't enough information to go on when you're the sheriff. If a player offers a lot of money to not open their bag, maybe they're bluffing and maybe not. If they offer nothing, maybe they're bluffing and maybe not. It's pretty much just a guessing game. I prefer Coup for a similar bluffing game experience because you at least have the card count (just 15 total cards) and the faceup cards of your opponents to help with deduction, and the diplomat can help give you even more information to go on. Plus it plays a lot faster.

I've seen several people complain about the end-game scoring and I really don't understand why. You see who has the most cards of each of the four types and you hand out money accordingly. Add up the value of all of your cards. Add that to your money. That's it.

This game can make for some fun moments, but it just doesn't hold up to repeated plays.

Sold.

RATING: 8
This game is a HUGE hit in my game group - to the extend where multiple people bought it. After seeing the guys from The Dice Tower play this game on a couple of their streams, I knew I had to buy it.

I do play with a slightly different rule, where you have 1 extra card in hand but aren't allowed to pick cards up from the discard pile. It makes the game slightly more random (meaning people have to lie more) and it speeds the game up tremendously, as people aren't constantly sifting through the discard piles.

Role-playing your role definitely adds a lot more flavour to this game. Without it, the game would still be great, just slightly less fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players take turns as the Sheriff of Nottingham. On your turn as sheriff, all the other players tell you how many cards they're putting into a sealed bag. They might be honest traders sending apples, bread, cheese or chickens. But they might also be sneaking contraband like silk, crossbows, etc. Your job as sheriff is to decide who's being dishonest and which bags to open. If you catch a smuggler, they lose the smuggled goods and pay you a hefty fine. If you harass an honest businessman, you have to compensate him for wasting his time.

As a trader you can bribe the sheriff to look the other way, or even to open someone else's bag. The main part of the game is bluffing or faking a bluff, and encouraging the sheriff to check other people's bags.

At the game end you get points for everything you shipped. There are awards for whoever ships the most of a particular type of good. And contraband items are generally a lot more valuable than basic goods.

This is an interesting game but it feels more like a party game for gamers than anything else.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
About [thing=157969][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=157969][/thing] is a set collection coupled with bluffing and negotiation. With the purpose of taking goods to Nottingham, players could simply be taking regular and legal goods. But they could also be bringing contraband, which by themselvers are worth much more in a 1-to-1 basis against the legal goods. However, if the Sheriff finds this, the merchant will have to pay a fine and will lose de contraband. While this is good for the Sheriff, his job isn't easy, after all, he is greedy and can't really trust people, so even when people are saying the truth about what they have in their bag, he might be inclined to open it and, then, he will be in a monerary problem, having to pay a fee to the honest merchant.

The game works with deals and traps made by and between the players. One can try to bluff the Sheriff into making him open her bag and pay a hefty fee, other may try to reffuse any deal, to appear 100% honest in his business, while carrying 4 contraband.

This is the game: bluff, double bluff, dares, deals, threats, vying for opportunities and measuring the ratio of danger and reward.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
One player in the round will be the Sheriff, all the others will be the merchants. Everyone has a hand of 6 cards and, in order, can trade up to 5 cards with those discarded in earlier rounds (at the start of the game some are discarded with this purpose). Then, every player puts from 1 to 5 cards of the same type, if legal, or of any type, if it is contraband, in her bag and say to the Sheriff what she is carrying - the player must name the exact quantity and name one legal good, even if she isn't carrying any, but if she does, she must say the name of this good. For instance:

P1 puts 3 apples in the bad. Says: "Im carrying 3 apples to the city, Sheriff."
P2 puts 2 breads and 1 contraband. Says: "Im carrying 3 breads to the city, Sheriff."
P3 puts 2 contrabands. Says: "Im carrying 2 chickens to the city, Sheriff."

In the inspection phase, the Sheriff can make deals with the merchants and open whatever bag he wants. If he makes a deal, he gives back the bag, without opening it - the merchant reveals the content of legal goods and put them on the proper area of the player board; contraband aren't totally revealed: they stay face down, but is possible to see the quantity. Therefore the Sheriff would know he got dupped.

If no deal is reached. The Sheriff can simply return, begrudgingly, the bag to a player, or opt to open it. If he does open, two things can happen:
a) The merchant player was saying the truth, so the Sheriff must pay a fee to the merchant in the value indicated in that good card. Say if the Sheriff opened the bag of P1. Those 3 apples would set the Sheriff back 6 coins (2 for each apple);
b) The merchant player was laying. The merchant pays a fine, in the value indicated in the contraband cards, to the Sheriff, and also loses the contraband cards, but the legal goods the merchant keep.

After this, the Sheriff token is passed to the next player, everyone draws cards until they have 6 cards in hand, and a new round will start.

Once everyone was Sheriff twice, the game ends. For each type of legal good the players will see who is the King (having the most of it) and the Queen (the second most), as these positions will earn extra points. All the goods are scored equal to their values, the same for the contraband (but these don't have a majority). The player with the most money will be the winner!

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
Going by round, the first is to trade or not. This tends to become less important as the game carries one, since the discard pile start to get filled with contraband. And people, while maybe wanting to take contraband, don't want to be seen by the Sheriff when drawing them! But, in the early rounds, helps a lot to create believable stories: if you take a Cheese from the discard, saying that you are carrying 4 Cheese sounds more likely than before.

Second is what to put in the bag. Both in quantity and in type. The higher the number, the harder is to convince the Sheriff that what you are taking is what you are saying it is. For most purposes, is better to fill the bag with all legal goods. You have no worries, no need to deal with the Sheriff, and gain the chance to bluff the Sheriff into opening your bag, and gaining the fee money. There is no downside. Well, at least until you don't want to simply carry 2 lousy apples. Since players can only take one type of legal good, sometimes you will have a hand of contraband and a sorted type of varied legal goods. Yes, you can even put a single card in the bag, but it is worth it? Usually it won't. So, eventually you might find yourself having to take contraband. First is necessary to calculate how much is worth. More is better, yes. But also calls for more attention, and the fine you will have to pay, if discovered, will be higher. It is not a hard thing to think about, but it is a decision.

Also, in this phase, is important to consider the majorities. It might be more worth to you to take that 2 lousy apples than 3 better chickens, simply because it could be enough to net you first or second place in the majority of apples. Still, more often than not that will be a decision that the blind draw will make for you.

Third, is in the negotiation with the Sheriff. Even when carrying all legal goods and having no need at all to negotiate, it can be worth. However, you will want to look suspicious and make the Sheriff open your bag, since, with this, he will need to pay you a fee for hassling your business. For the merchant carrying contraband, the same is valid, as being suspicious can work as a double bluff: are you trying to look suspicious or are you indeed a criminal? Since several times players are just trying to trap the Sheriff, is hard for it to separate the good from the bad.

For the Sheriff all of his decisions are in this negotation phase. Open or not. Pondering about the amounts (considering what was already revealed and the total in the deck), about the risk and reward of opening a bag. Trying to squeeze as much as possible, both of money and of information, from the merchants. It is not esasy, specially because you can have a roun in which everyone is taking legal goods, and the only option that is good to you is to let everyone pass - but considering that players are only Sheriff twice, it is very, very hard to not open at least one bag each time. A good Sheriff's turn is when you don't lose money.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Great production value, going from art, quality of cards and of the players boards;
- High interaction between players;
- Language independant;
- Small set of rules and easy to start playing;
- It is very fun to see people talking their way out or into situations.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- The increase in value for the legal goods, due to the majorities, made the game less of a negotiation and more of traps;
- One can win by simply passing legal goods all the time, without any risk whatsoever;
- The box is huge when compared with that of [thing=104640][/thing], its antecessor.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Mostly like going through customs while carrying way above the limit of goods to enter back in the country after a trip. Should you declare it? You will pay a fee, but all will be good - but you might feel you just lost, since you could have entered without spending a dime more than you already did. Even more, the money is on top of some already way too high taxes you pay for everything, and you don't even agree with the way it is spent, when it is spent, after all, most is simply "lost" due to corruption anyway. You would be basically giving money to thieves. 

Well, maybe in your country isn't like this. But [thing=157969][/thing] was, first, [thing=104640][/thing], which, in turn, was [thing=22237][/thing], which was [thing=3705][/thing]. When Sérgio Halaban and André Zatz, the designers, took [thing=22237][/thing], they did as to look like the border between Brazil and Paraguay - a place where, in Ciudad Del Este, they sell a lot of, well, contraband. It was (and still is, but way less due to the decrease of value of our currency in face of the dollar) usual for business in Brazil to go there to buy lots of illegal goods and bring them into Brazil. That border was a roulette. Some will get pull to the side and get caught, and would either lose the goods or would need to pay a fee. Most would pass. It was a gamble, and was known that some workers - of customs and police - could be paid to "look the other way", but these was only true for the big things, as small business people and individual folk would risk in the roulette.

[thing=157969][/thing] is like this, only in a much more innocent setting, which can even make the contraband a way to stick to the evil rulers, to help the rebellion. Better for families. 

I would have liked [thing=157969][/thing] more if I didn't already know [thing=104640][/thing]. [thing=157969][/thing] is a fun game, sure. But one, for me, inferior to [thing=104640][/thing], as it made the legal goods too valuable, enough for players to win only with them. This is nice, when playing a friendly game, with, say, family. In [thing=104640][/thing] players need to pass contraband, or they will lose. This means that there were a LOT more laying, bluffing, and deals being made. There is much more tension and fun in [thing=104640][/thing]. [thing=157969][/thing] is just a waterdown version. But is the one you will find to sell, and is good enough on its own right - it isn't bad because is worst than [thing=104640][/thing], because [thing=104640][/thing] is very good.

If you can, find [thing=104640][/thing], go for it. It is better, and comes in a box around 6x times smaller, while carrying basically the same game. Still, if you can't, [thing=157969][/thing] will do just fine.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Super fun game to play if your group is in the "right mood". Most important thing to notice is that at least in my opinion this is actually not a bluffing game but rather a negotiation game. Basically this means that the Sheriff should NOT always open the bag if he knows that there is contraband but rather make a deal. This encourages bluffing and deeper strategy since if the Sheriffs open bags very easily it means that the guys who always tell the truth win basically every time. If that happens the game easily devolves into terrible game (since telling the truth is only luck of the draw -> game turns into a coin flip contest where lying is not an option). Works very well with some drinking involved. I think that my rating will go up if the upcoming expansion makes the lying more profitable.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A bluffing game at its finest. When it's your turn as the Sheriff, you have to try to figure out who (if anyone,) is trying to smuggle in contraband. The back-and-forth of bribe offering and tension as you decide whether or not to open someone's bag is really fun and interesting. Same when you are a merchant. Do you risk trying to smuggle in a bunch of contraband this round or play it safe and put only legal goods in your bag and hope you can get the Sheriff to open it? All of that decision-making and negotiating makes this an amazing game.

RATING: 6
SoN is essentially a bluffing game that tries to incorporate elements of deal making, bribery and backstabbing in the design. There are shades of Intrigue in SoN but it doesn't work nearly as well. Any bribes, promises or paybacks can be carried over between rounds but does not need to be honored. However, this doesn't seem to work nearly as well in SoN as most deals played out in the present round and not carried forward. Overall, the negotiation phase is more structured than Intrigue and hence, fewer opportunities to walk back on your promises. 

The game does drag a little in terms of negotiations and can get bogged down if not moving at a fast enough pace. Meta game is probably important and playing with the same folks a few times can establish a pattern. Taking risks in contraband appears very important for winning the game as the points obtained from contraband is high. It is tough being Sheriff as you get penalized for inspecting bags for bona fide goods and most of the time, the contraband payoff is just not as lucrative to offset the penalties. This makes taking bribes the default path.

This game will work for some and not for others. Folks who hate lying, cheating and backstabbing will likely not love the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Nice, pure game of bluff and double-cross.  The game itself is little more than a sparse framework to lie to your friends.  It's like playing poker, except that virtually every hand is a crap hand.  The sheriff player has to do a combination of deduction, people-reading, and just plain luck to get his way.  You can choose to skim a little off the top, usually, but does that mean they're just going to get through with a huge amount of contraband?

I've had this game taught to me two different ways.  One had much harsher penalties for lying.  I'm not sure which was the correct way, but the framework worked for both sessions, so I think it's a good game regardless.

The game does sometimes outstay its welcome, but it's so pure that it will keep most games going well.  If you want to make sure the game stays fast-paced, try doing a practice sheriff round so that the players know what they're doing, and then only do the Sheriff role once around the table rather than twice.

And, uh, what does this game have to do with Robin Hood?  The original theme made more sense.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I haven't laughed this hard playing a game in a very long time. I've heard it said that when the players are providing the entertainment, it's a sign of a weak game. While I see the wisdom in that, I'm not sure it applies to Sheriff of Nottingham.

The reason is that this game can be mathed out. It is easy to calculate payouts for any given bag, fairly easy to track what cards are in play and certainly easy enough to calculate profitable bribes for both sides, so there is a solid economic game here. Above the table though, separated from all that, the stage is set for comedic gold. Watching people try to negotiate, bribe, cajole, justify and flat out beg for favors is unbelievably entertaining.

I think this is worth a 10 rating, fun wise. I'm going to low ball it a little, because I suspect that the luster will fade a bit when played repeatedly with the same group. (I think all social engineering games tend toward this, even Battlestar Galactica and The Resistance start to lose something once you learn everyone's tendencies.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Sheriff of Nottingham is a social deduction game with some negotiation thrown in. Players will take on the roles of merchants that are trying to sell their wares, while also tolerating the strict laws and bans of various goods. One player plays as the sheriff each round, who will try and gauge whether or not players are lying about the contents of their bags. A large appeal to this game is the fact that players do not have to lie to win. In fact, it is very likely that players can win while playing honestly the whole session. This is due to the penalties that are placed on the sheriff for investigating an innocent player, as well as the bonuses for accumulating large amounts of legal goods.

RATING: 8
Players take turn being the Sheriff. Other players, acting as Merchants, will attempt to bring their goods into the city for profit. Beware though, while many may act as honest merchants, there is always the possibility of contraband being smuggled into the city!
Merchants can try to bribe the sheriff, to not inspect their goods, but if he finds contraband they have to pay a fine. If they were honest merchantsthe sheriff has to pay for the inconvenience.
Also known as "Customs" or "What's in the bag you bastard!"

RATING: 7
I take most reviews with a grain of salt, and as such I take most review[b]ers[/b] with a grain of salt as well, but when it comes to his favorite game, Tom Vasel and I see eye-to-eye. So when he says he's launching a line of games that he considers [i]essential[/i], he has my attention. I saw this come up for pre-order and Gen Con pickup and I handed over my money fast.

After playing it once now, I can see why Tom wanted this in his Dice Tower Essentials. It's a quick game, easy to teach, and leads to some interesting decisions and fun bluffing. I really do like how you can play honest and win, but if there's enough contraband trading going on, you might have issues which creates [i]just enough[/i] pressure.

I'm not sure there are many downsides to the game other than you should understand that it's mostly about bluffing, so if that's not your thing, you probably won't have a good time. Also, be sure that you're paying attention to the sets and are trying to achieve king or queen because diversification won't win you the game, it'll only let you bluff a bit better.

RATING: N/A
Players are merchants bringing into Nottinghame apples, cheese, bread, chickens and contraband (if they can get away with it). Each round the players take turns playing the role of the Sheriff whose responsibility is to keep contraband out of the city and make sure merchants are bringing into town what they declare. Extra points for food majorities. Catchy, fun, and light weight card game with great interaction through bluffing, looking for player tells, and negotiating with bribes. You can even bribe the sheriff to check the other player's packet. Will you crack under the careful scrutiny of the sheriff, threatening to open your packet?

RATING: 8
Bluffing at its finest. Simple and fun. You have four different types of legal goods and four different types of illegal goods, as well as special illegal goods. You may put whatever you like in the bag and then you must claim only one type of legal good. You could just be honest. But, c'mon, no one stays honest in this game. I love being the sheriff and also getting contraband past the sheriff. This is another game I think everyone should own.
